<img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" width="10"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template talk:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Philosophy_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Metaphysics</b> is the branch of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that studies the fundamental nature of reality, the first principles of being, identity and change, space and time, causality, necessity, and possibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> It includes questions about the nature of <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a> and the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a>, between <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">attribute</a>, and between <a href="/wiki/Potentiality_and_actuality" title="Potentiality and actuality">potentiality and actuality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The word "metaphysics" comes from two Greek words that, together, literally mean "after or behind or among [the study of] the natural". It has been suggested that the term might have been coined by a first century CE editor who assembled various small selections of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s works into the treatise we now know by the name <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)"><i>Metaphysics</i></a> (μετὰ τὰ φυσικά, <i>meta ta physika</i>, <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr> 'after the <a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)"><i>Physics</i></a> ', another of Aristotle's works).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford.Enc.Philosophy_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford.Enc.Philosophy-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Metaphysics studies questions related to what it is for something to exist and what types of existence there are. Metaphysics seeks to answer, in an abstract and fully general manner, the questions of:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>What <em>there is</em></li>
<li>What it is <em>like</em></li></ul>
<p>Topics of metaphysical investigation include <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object (philosophy)">objects</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">properties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a>, <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">cause and effect</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Subjunctive_possibility" title="Subjunctive possibility">possibility</a>. Metaphysics is considered one of the four main branches of philosophy, along with <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word "metaphysics" derives from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> words <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BC%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%AC" title="wikt:μετά">μετά</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Meta" title="Meta">metá</a></i>, "after") and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%86%CF%85%CF%83%CE%B9%CE%BA%CE%AC" title="wikt:φυσικά">φυσικά</a> (<i>physiká</i>, "physics").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> It was first used as the title for several of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s works, because they were usually anthologized after the works on <a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">physics</a> in complete editions. The prefix <i>meta-</i> ("after") indicates that these works come "after" the chapters on physics. However, Aristotle himself did not call the subject of these books metaphysics: he referred to it as "<b>first philosophy</b>" (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">πρώτη φιλοσοφία</span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">philosophia prima</i>). The editor of Aristotle's works, <a href="/wiki/Andronicus_of_Rhodes" title="Andronicus of Rhodes">Andronicus of Rhodes</a>, is thought to have placed the books on first philosophy right after another work, <i>Physics</i>, and called them <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">τὰ μετὰ τὰ φυσικὰ βιβλία</span></span> (<i>tà metà tà physikà biblía</i>) or "the books [that come] after the [books on] physics".
</p><p>However, once the name was given, the commentators sought to find other reasons for its appropriateness. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> understood it to refer to the chronological or pedagogical order among our philosophical studies, so that the "metaphysical sciences" would mean "those that we study after having mastered the sciences that deal with the physical world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term was misread by other medieval commentators, who thought it meant "the science of what is beyond the physical".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Following this tradition, the prefix <i>meta-</i> has more recently been prefixed to the names of sciences to designate higher sciences dealing with ulterior and more fundamental problems: hence <a href="/wiki/Metamathematics" title="Metamathematics">metamathematics</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/metaphysiology" title="wikt:metaphysiology">metaphysiology</a>, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>A person who creates or develops metaphysical theories is called a <i>metaphysician</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Common parlance also uses the word <i>metaphysics</i> for a different referent from that of those already mentioned, namely for beliefs in arbitrary non-physical or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magic_(paranormal)" title="Magic (paranormal)">magical</a> entities. For example, "metaphysical healing" to refer to healing by means of remedies that are magical rather than scientific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> This usage stemmed from the various historical schools of speculative metaphysics which operated by postulating all manner of physical, mental and spiritual entities as bases for particular metaphysical systems. Metaphysics as a subject does not preclude beliefs in such magical entities but neither does it promote them. Rather, it is the subject which provides the vocabulary and logic with which such beliefs might be analyzed and studied, for example to search for inconsistencies both within themselves and with other accepted systems such as <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemological_foundation">Epistemological foundation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Epistemological foundation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">Epistemology</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2018</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Metaphysical study is conducted using <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deduction</a> from that which is known <i><a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">a priori</a></i>. Like <a href="/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">foundational mathematics</a> (which is sometimes considered a special case of metaphysics applied to the existence of number), it tries to give a coherent account of the structure of the world, capable of explaining our everyday and scientific perception of the world, and being free from contradictions. In mathematics, there are many different ways to define numbers; similarly, in metaphysics, there are many different ways to define objects, properties, concepts, and other entities that are claimed to make up the world. While metaphysics may, as a special case, study the entities postulated by fundamental science such as atoms and superstrings, its core topic is the set of categories such as object, property and causality which those scientific theories assume. For example: claiming that "electrons have charge" is a scientific theory; while exploring what it means for electrons to be (or at least, to be perceived as) "objects", charge to be a "property", and for both to exist in a topological entity called "space" is the task of metaphysics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two broad stances about what is "the world" studied by metaphysics. According to <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_realism" title="Philosophical realism">metaphysical realism</a>, the objects studied by metaphysics exist independently of any observer so that the subject is the most fundamental of all sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khlentzos_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Khlentzos-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anti-realism#Metaphysical_anti-realism" title="Anti-realism">Metaphysical anti-realism</a>, on the other hand, assumes that the objects studied by metaphysics exist inside the mind of an observer, so the subject becomes a form of <a href="/wiki/Introspection" title="Introspection">introspection</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conceptual_analysis" title="Conceptual analysis">conceptual analysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khlentzos_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Khlentzos-13">[13]</a></sup> This position is of more recent origin. Some philosophers, notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kant" title="Kant">Kant</a>, discuss both of these "worlds" and what can be inferred about each one. Some, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logical_positivists" title="Logical positivists">logical positivists</a>, and many scientists, reject the metaphysical realism as meaningless and unverifiable. Others reply that this criticism also applies to any type of knowledge, including hard science, which claims to describe anything other than the contents of human perception, and thus that the world of perception <i>is</i> the objective world in some sense. Metaphysics itself usually assumes that some stance has been taken on these questions and that it may proceed independently of the choice—the question of which stance to take belongs instead to another branch of philosophy, <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_questions">Central questions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Central questions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Ontology_.28being.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ontology_(being)">Ontology (being)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ontology (being)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">Ontology</a></div>
<p><i>Ontology</i> is the branch of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that studies concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">being</a>, <a href="/wiki/Becoming_(philosophy)" title="Becoming (philosophy)">becoming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a>. It includes the questions of how entities are grouped into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Category_of_being" title="Category of being">basic categories</a> and which of these entities exist on the most fundamental level. Ontology is sometimes referred to as the <i>science of being</i>. It has been characterized as <i>general metaphysics</i> in contrast to <i>special metaphysics</i>, which is concerned with more particular aspects of being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Ontologists often try to determine what the <i>categories</i> or <i>highest kinds</i> are and how they form a <i>system of categories</i> that provides an encompassing classification of all entities. Commonly proposed categories include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substance_(philosophy)" title="Substance (philosophy)">substances</a>, <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">properties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Relations_(philosophy)" title="Relations (philosophy)">relations</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/States_of_affairs" title="States of affairs">states of affairs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Event_(philosophy)" title="Event (philosophy)">events</a>. These categories are characterized by fundamental ontological concepts, like <i>particularity</i> and <i>universality</i>, <i>abstractness</i> and <i>concreteness</i> or <i>possibility</i> and <i>necessity</i>. Of special interest is the concept of <i>ontological dependence</i>, which determines whether the entities of a category exist on the <i>most fundamental level</i>. Disagreements within ontology are often about whether entities belonging to a certain category exist and, if so, how they are related to other entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity_and_change">Identity and change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Identity and change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Identity_(philosophy)" title="Identity (philosophy)">Identity (philosophy)</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Identity_(philosophy)" title="Identity (philosophy)">Identity</a> is a fundamental metaphysical concern. Metaphysicians investigating identity are tasked with the question of what, exactly, it means for something to be identical to itself, or – more controversially – to something else. Issues of identity arise in the context of <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a>: what does it mean for something to be itself across two moments in time? How do we account for this? Another question of identity arises when we ask what our criteria ought to be for determining identity, and how the reality of identity interfaces with linguistic expressions.
</p><p>The metaphysical positions one takes on identity have far-reaching implications on issues such as the <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem" title="Mind–body problem">mind–body problem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Personal_identity" title="Personal identity">personal identity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Law_(principle)" title="Law (principle)">law</a>.
</p><p>A few ancient Greeks took extreme positions on the nature of change. <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a> denied change altogether, while <a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a> argued that change was ubiquitous: "No man ever steps in the same river twice."
</p><p>Identity, sometimes called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Numerical_identity" title="Numerical identity">numerical identity</a>, is the relation that a thing bears to itself, and which no thing bears to anything other than itself (cf. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sameness" title="Sameness">sameness</a>).
</p><p>A modern philosopher who made a lasting impact on the philosophy of identity was <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a>, whose <i>law of the indiscernibility of identicals</i> is still widely accepted today. It states that if some object <i>x</i> is identical to some object <i>y</i>, then any property that <i>x</i> has, <i>y</i> will have as well.
</p><p>Put formally, it states
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \forall x\;\forall y\;(x=y\rightarrow \forall P\;(P(x)\leftrightarrow P(y)))}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">∀<!-- ∀ --></mi>
<mi>x</mi>
<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∀<!-- ∀ --></mi>
<mi>y</mi>
<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∀<!-- ∀ --></mi>
<mi>P</mi>
<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>P</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">↔<!-- ↔ --></mo>
<mi>P</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \forall x\;\forall y\;(x=y\rightarrow \forall P\;(P(x)\leftrightarrow P(y)))}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \forall x\;\forall y\;(x=y\rightarrow \forall P\;(P(x)\leftrightarrow P(y)))}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e3a9c8d909378818d94a1ab8501e2569eeaac5a" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:36.069ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>However, it does seem that objects can change over time. Two rival theories to account for the relationship between change and identity are <i><a href="/wiki/Perdurantism" title="Perdurantism">perdurantism</a></i>, which treats objects as a series of object-stages, and <i><a href="/wiki/Endurantism" title="Endurantism">endurantism</a></i>, which maintains that the organism—the same object—is present at every stage in its history.
</p><p>By appealing to <a href="/wiki/Intrinsic_and_extrinsic_properties_(philosophy)" title="Intrinsic and extrinsic properties (philosophy)">intrinsic and extrinsic properties</a>, endurantism finds a way to harmonize identity with change. Endurantists believe that objects persist by being strictly numerically identical over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-19">[19]</a></sup> However, if Leibniz's law of the indiscernibility of identicals is utilized to define numerical identity here, it seems that objects must be completely unchanged in order to persist. Discriminating between intrinsic properties and extrinsic properties, endurantists state that numerical identity means that, if some object <i>x</i> is identical to some object <i>y</i>, then any <i>intrinsic</i> property that <i>x</i> has, <i>y</i> will have as well. Thus, if an object persists, <i>intrinsic</i> properties of it are unchanged, but <i>extrinsic</i> properties can change over time. Besides the object itself, environments and other objects can change over time; properties that relate to other objects would change even if this object does not change.
</p><p>Perdurantism can harmonize identity with change in another way. In <a href="/wiki/Four-dimensionalism" title="Four-dimensionalism">four-dimensionalism</a>, a version of perdurantism, what persists is a four-dimensional object which does not change although three-dimensional slices of the object may differ.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_and_time">Space and time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Space and time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_space_and_time" title="Philosophy of space and time">Philosophy of space and time</a></div>
<p>Objects appear to us in space and time, while abstract entities such as classes, properties, and relations do not. How do space and time serve this function as a ground for objects? Are space and time entities themselves, of some form? Must they exist prior to objects? How exactly can they be defined? How is time related to change; must there always be something changing in order for time to exist?
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Causality">Causality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Causality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">Causality</a></div>
<p>Classical philosophy recognized a number of causes, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleological" title="Teleological">teleological</a> final causes. In <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a> the notions of space, time and causality become tangled together, with temporal orders of causations becoming dependent on who is observing them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Temporal order can be observer-dependent, but not between causally related events. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The laws of physics are symmetrical in time, so could equally well be used to describe time as running backwards. Why then do we perceive it as flowing in one direction, the <a href="/wiki/Arrow_of_time" title="Arrow of time">arrow of time</a>, and as containing causation flowing in the same direction?
</p><p>For that matter, can an effect precede its cause? This was the title of a 1954 paper by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dummett" title="Michael Dummett">Michael Dummett</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> which sparked a discussion that continues today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Earlier, in 1947, <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">C. S. Lewis</a> had argued that one can meaningfully pray concerning the outcome of, e.g., a medical test while recognizing that the outcome is determined by past events: "My free act contributes to the cosmic shape."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Likewise, some interpretations of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, dating to 1945, involve backward-in-time causal influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">Causality</a> is linked by many philosophers to the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counterfactuals" title="Counterfactuals">counterfactuals</a>. To say that A caused B means that if A had not happened then B would not have happened. This view was advanced by <a href="/wiki/David_Lewis_(philosopher)" title="David Lewis (philosopher)">David Lewis</a> in his 1973 paper "Causation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> His subsequent papers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> further develop his theory of causation.
</p><p>Causality is usually required as a foundation for <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">philosophy of science</a> if science aims to understand causes and effects and make predictions about them.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Necessity_and_possibility">Necessity and possibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Necessity and possibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">Modal logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modal_realism" title="Modal realism">Modal realism</a></div>
<p>Metaphysicians investigate questions about the ways the world could have been. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Kellogg_Lewis" title="David Kellogg Lewis">David Lewis</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Plurality_of_Worlds" title="On the Plurality of Worlds">On the Plurality of Worlds</a></i>, endorsed a view called concrete <a href="/wiki/Modal_realism" title="Modal realism">modal realism</a>, according to which facts about how things could have been are made true by other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concrete_(philosophy)" title="Concrete (philosophy)">concrete</a> worlds in which things are different. Other philosophers, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a>, have dealt with the idea of possible worlds as well. A necessary fact is true across all <a href="/wiki/Possible_world" title="Possible world">possible worlds</a>. A possible fact is true in some possible world, even if not in the actual world. For example, it is possible that cats could have had two tails, or that any particular apple could have not existed. By contrast, certain propositions seem necessarily true, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analytic_proposition" title="Analytic proposition">analytic propositions</a>, e.g., "All bachelors are unmarried." The view that any <a href="/wiki/Logical_truth" title="Logical truth">analytic truth</a> is necessary is not universally held among philosophers. A less controversial view is that self-identity is necessary, as it seems fundamentally incoherent to claim that any <i>x</i> is not identical to itself; this is known as the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_identity" title="Law of identity">law of identity</a>, a putative "first principle". Similarly, Aristotle describes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_non-contradiction" title="Law of non-contradiction">principle of non-contradiction</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>It is impossible that the same quality should both belong and not belong to the same thing ... This is the most certain of all principles ... Wherefore they who demonstrate refer to this as an ultimate opinion. For it is by nature the source of all the other axioms.</dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Peripheral_questions">Peripheral questions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Peripheral questions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysical_cosmology_and_cosmogony">Metaphysical cosmology and cosmogony</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Metaphysical cosmology and cosmogony">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmology_(philosophy)" title="Cosmology (philosophy)">Cosmology (philosophy)</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmology_(philosophy)" title="Cosmology (philosophy)">Metaphysical cosmology</a> is the branch of metaphysics that deals with the <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">world</a> as the totality of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomena" title="Phenomena">phenomena</a> in <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a>. Historically, it formed a major part of the subject alongside <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontology</a>, though its role is more peripheral in contemporary philosophy. It has had a broad scope, and in many cases was founded in religion. The ancient Greeks drew no distinction between this use and their model for the cosmos. However, in modern times it addresses questions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> which are beyond the scope of the physical sciences. It is distinguished from religious cosmology in that it approaches these questions using philosophical methods (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Dialectic" title="Dialectic">dialectics</a>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cosmogony" title="Cosmogony">Cosmogony</a> deals specifically with the origin of the universe. Modern metaphysical cosmology and cosmogony try to address questions such as:
</p>
<ul><li>What is the origin of the Universe? What is its first cause? Is its existence necessary? (see <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emanationism" title="Emanationism">emanationism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Creationism" title="Creationism">creationism</a>)</li>
<li>What are the ultimate material components of the Universe? (see <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_(philosophy)" title="Mechanism (philosophy)">mechanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dynamism_(metaphysics)" title="Dynamism (metaphysics)">dynamism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">hylomorphism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomism</a>)</li>
<li>What is the ultimate reason for the existence of the Universe? Does the cosmos have a purpose? (see <a href="/wiki/Teleology" title="Teleology">teleology</a>)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mind_and_matter">Mind and matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mind and matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">Philosophy of mind</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dualism-vs-Monism.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="356" data-file-width="651" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Dualism-vs-Monism.png/350px-Dualism-vs-Monism.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Dualism-vs-Monism.png/525px-Dualism-vs-Monism.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Dualism-vs-Monism.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dualism-vs-Monism.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Different approaches toward resolving the mind–body problem.</div></div></div>
<p>Accounting for the existence of <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> in a world largely composed of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> is a metaphysical problem which is so large and important as to have become a specialized subject of study in its own right, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substance_dualism" title="Substance dualism">Substance dualism</a> is a classical theory in which mind and body are essentially different, with the mind having some of the attributes traditionally assigned to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" title="Soul (spirit)">soul</a>, and which creates an immediate conceptual puzzle about how the two interact. This form of substance dualism differs from the dualism of some eastern philosophical traditions (like Nyāya), which also posit a soul; for the soul, under their view, is ontologically distinct from the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">Idealism</a> postulates that material objects do not exist unless perceived and only as perceptions. Adherents of <a href="/wiki/Panpsychism" title="Panpsychism">panpsychism</a>, a kind of <a href="/wiki/Property_dualism" title="Property dualism">property dualism</a>, hold that everything <i>has</i> a mental aspect, but not that everything exists <i>in</i> a mind. <a href="/wiki/Neutral_monism" title="Neutral monism">Neutral monism</a> postulates that existence consists of a single substance that in itself is neither mental nor physical, but is capable of mental and physical aspects or attributes – thus it implies a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dual-aspect_theory" title="Dual-aspect theory">dual-aspect theory</a>. For the last century, the dominant theories have been science-inspired including <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialistic monism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Identity_theory_of_mind" title="Identity theory of mind">type identity theory</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Token_identity" title="Token identity">token identity theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Functionalism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Functionalism (philosophy of mind)">functionalism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reductive_physicalism" title="Reductive physicalism">reductive physicalism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonreductive_physicalism" title="Nonreductive physicalism">nonreductive physicalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eliminative_materialism" title="Eliminative materialism">eliminative materialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anomalous_monism" title="Anomalous monism">anomalous monism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Property_dualism" title="Property dualism">property dualism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epiphenomenalism" title="Epiphenomenalism">epiphenomenalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emergentism" title="Emergentism">emergentism</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Determinism_and_free_will">Determinism and free will</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Determinism and free will">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">Determinism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">Free will</a></div>
<p>Determinism is the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical</a> <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a> that every event, including human cognition, decision and action, is <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causally</a> determined by an unbroken chain of prior occurrences. It holds that nothing happens that has not already been determined. The principal consequence of the deterministic claim is that it poses a challenge to the existence of free will.
</p><p>The problem of free will is the problem of whether rational agents exercise control over their own actions and decisions. Addressing this problem requires understanding the relation between freedom and causation, and determining whether the laws of nature are causally deterministic. Some philosophers, known as <a href="/wiki/Incompatibilism" title="Incompatibilism">incompatibilists</a>, view determinism and free will as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutually_exclusive" title="Mutually exclusive">mutually exclusive</a>. If they believe in determinism, they will therefore believe free will to be an illusion, a position known as <i>hard determinism</i>. Proponents range from <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ted_Honderich" title="Ted Honderich">Ted Honderich</a>. <a href="/wiki/Henri_Bergson" title="Henri Bergson">Henri Bergson</a> defended free will in his dissertation <i><a href="/wiki/Time_and_Free_Will" title="Time and Free Will">Time and Free Will</a></i> from 1889.
</p><p>Others, labeled <a href="/wiki/Compatibilism" title="Compatibilism">compatibilists</a> (or "soft determinists"), believe that the two ideas can be reconciled coherently. Adherents of this view include <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> and many modern philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Martin_Fischer" title="John Martin Fischer">John Martin Fischer</a>, Gary Watson, <a href="/wiki/Harry_Frankfurt" title="Harry Frankfurt">Harry Frankfurt</a>, and the like.
</p><p>Incompatibilists who accept free will but reject determinism are called <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_(metaphysics)" title="Libertarianism (metaphysics)">libertarians</a>, a term not to be confused with the political sense. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kane_(philosopher)" title="Robert Kane (philosopher)">Robert Kane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Plantinga" title="Alvin Plantinga">Alvin Plantinga</a> are modern defenders of this theory.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_and_social_kinds">Natural and social kinds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Natural and social kinds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The earliest type of classification of social construction traces back to <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> in his dialogue <a href="/wiki/Phaedrus_(dialogue)" title="Phaedrus (dialogue)">Phaedrus</a> where he claims that the biological classification system seems to carve nature at the joints. In contrast, later philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Michel Foucault</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">Jorge Luis Borges</a> have challenged the capacity of natural and social classification. In his essay <a href="/wiki/The_Analytical_Language_of_John_Wilkins" title="The Analytical Language of John Wilkins">The Analytical Language of John Wilkins</a>, Borges makes us imagine a certain encyclopedia where the animals are divided into (a) those that belong to the emperor; (b) embalmed ones; (c) those that are trained; ... and so forth, in order to bring forward the ambiguity of natural and social kinds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> According to metaphysics author Alyssa Ney: "The reason all this is interesting is that there seems to be a metaphysical difference between the Borgesian system and Plato's".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The difference is not obvious but one classification attempts to carve entities up according to objective distinction while the other does not. According to <a href="/wiki/Willard_Van_Orman_Quine" title="Willard Van Orman Quine">Quine</a> this notion is closely related to the notion of similarity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_social_science" title="Philosophy of social science">philosopher of social science</a> <a href="/wiki/Jason_Josephson_Storm" title="Jason Josephson Storm">Jason Josephson Storm</a> has attempted to provide a more precise definition of social kinds, arguing that social kinds may still be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Realism_(philosophical)" title="Realism (philosophical)">real</a> insofar as they are determined by empricially observable causal processes and that many cases of what appear to be natural kinds — including biological natural kinds and the category of "natural kind" itself — are in fact social kinds; such a view would mitigate the need to prioritize natural kinds above social kinds for much scientific practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Number">Number</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Number">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mathematics" title="Philosophy of mathematics">Philosophy of mathematics</a></div>
<p>There are different ways to set up the notion of number in metaphysics theories. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonist" title="Platonist">Platonist</a> theories postulate number as a fundamental category itself. Others consider it to be a property of an entity called a "group" comprising other entities; or to be a relation held between several groups of entities, such as "the number four is the set of all sets of four things". Many of the debates around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universals" title="Universals">universals</a> are applied to the study of number, and are of particular importance due to its status as a foundation for the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mathematics" title="Philosophy of mathematics">philosophy of mathematics</a> and for <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> itself.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Applied_metaphysics">Applied metaphysics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Applied metaphysics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although metaphysics as a philosophical enterprise is highly hypothetical, it also has practical application in most other branches of philosophy, science, and now also information technology. Such areas generally assume some basic ontology (such as a system of objects, properties, classes, and space-time) as well as other metaphysical stances on topics such as causality and agency, then build their own particular theories upon these.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, for example, some theories are based on the ontological assumption of objects with properties (such as electrons having charge) while others may reject objects completely (such as quantum field theories, where spread-out "electronness" becomes property of space-time rather than an object).
</p><p>"Social" branches of philosophy such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_morality" title="Philosophy of morality">philosophy of morality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_religion" title="Philosophy of religion">philosophy of religion</a> (which in turn give rise to practical subjects such as ethics, politics, law, and art) all require metaphysical foundations, which may be considered as branches or applications of metaphysics. For example, they may postulate the existence of basic entities such as value, beauty, and God. Then they use these postulates to make their own arguments about consequences resulting from them. When philosophers in these subjects make their foundations they are doing applied metaphysics, and may draw upon its core topics and methods to guide them, including ontology and other core and peripheral topics. As in science, the foundations chosen will in turn depend on the underlying ontology used, so philosophers in these subjects may have to dig right down to the ontological layer of metaphysics to find what is possible for their theories.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_other_disciplines">Relation to other disciplines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Relation to other disciplines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prior to the modern <a href="/wiki/History_of_science" title="History of science">history of science</a>, scientific questions were addressed as a part of <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>. Originally, the term "science" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">scientia</i>) simply meant "knowledge". The <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>, however, transformed natural philosophy into an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical" title="Empirical">empirical</a> activity deriving from <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiment</a>, unlike the rest of philosophy. By the end of the 18th century, it had begun to be called "science" to distinguish it from other branches of philosophy. Science and philosophy have been considered separated disciplines ever since. Thereafter, metaphysics denoted philosophical enquiry of a non-empirical character into the nature of existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_Gay_pp._132-141_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Gay_pp._132-141-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Metaphysics continues asking "why" where science leaves off. For example, any theory of fundamental physics is based on some set of <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a>, which may postulate the existence of entities such as atoms, particles, forces, charges, mass, or fields. Stating such postulates is considered to be the "end" of a science theory. Metaphysics takes these postulates and explores what they mean as human concepts. For example, do all theories of physics require the existence of space and time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> objects, and properties? Or can they be expressed using only objects, or only properties? Do the objects have to retain their identity over time or can they change?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> If they change, then are they still the same object? Can theories be reformulated by converting properties or predicates (such as "red") into entities (such as redness or redness fields) or processes ('there is some redding happening over there' appears in some human languages in place of the use of properties). Is the distinction between objects and properties fundamental to the physical world or to our perception of it?
</p><p>Much recent work has been devoted to analyzing the role of metaphysics in scientific theorizing. <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Koyr%C3%A9" title="Alexandre Koyré">Alexandre Koyré</a> led this movement, declaring in his book <i>Metaphysics and Measurement</i>, "It is not by following experiment, but by outstripping experiment, that the scientific mind makes progress."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> That metaphysical propositions can influence scientific theorizing is <a href="/wiki/John_W._N._Watkins" title="John W. N. Watkins">John Watkins</a>' most lasting contribution to philosophy. Since 1957<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> "he showed the ways in which some un-testable and hence, according to <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Popperian</a> ideas, non-empirical propositions can nevertheless be influential in the development of properly testable and hence scientific theories. These profound results in applied elementary logic...represented an important corrective to positivist teachings about the meaninglessness of metaphysics and of normative claims".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fred_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fred-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Imre_Lakatos" title="Imre Lakatos">Imre Lakatos</a> maintained that all scientific theories have a metaphysical "hard core" essential for the generation of hypotheses and theoretical assumptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Thus, according to Lakatos, "scientific changes are connected with vast cataclysmic metaphysical revolutions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>An example from biology of Lakatos' thesis: <a href="/wiki/David_Hull_(philosopher)" title="David Hull (philosopher)">David Hull</a> has argued that changes in the ontological status of the species concept have been central in the development of biological thought from <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> through <a href="/wiki/Georges_Cuvier" title="Georges Cuvier">Cuvier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste Lamarck">Lamarck</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Darwin</a>. Darwin's ignorance of metaphysics made it more difficult for him to respond to his critics because he could not readily grasp the ways in which their underlying metaphysical views differed from his own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>In physics, new metaphysical ideas have arisen in connection with <a href="/wiki/Introduction_to_quantum_mechanics" title="Introduction to quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, where subatomic particles arguably do not have the same sort of individuality as the particulars with which philosophy has traditionally been concerned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Also, adherence to a deterministic metaphysics in the face of the challenge posed by the quantum-mechanical <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">uncertainty principle</a> led physicists such as Albert Einstein to propose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hidden_variable_theory" title="Hidden variable theory">alternative theories</a> that retained determinism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">A.N. Whitehead</a> is famous for creating a <a href="/wiki/Process_philosophy" title="Process philosophy">process philosophy</a> metaphysics inspired by electromagnetism and special relativity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In chemistry, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilbert_Newton_Lewis" title="Gilbert Newton Lewis">Gilbert Newton Lewis</a> addressed the nature of motion, arguing that an electron should not be said to move when it has none of the properties of motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Katherine Hawley notes that the metaphysics even of a widely accepted scientific theory may be challenged if it can be argued that the metaphysical presuppositions of the theory make no contribution to its predictive success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is a relationship between theological doctrines and philosophical reflection in the philosophy of a religion (such as <a href="/wiki/Christian_philosophy" title="Christian philosophy">Christian philosophy</a>); philosophical reflections are strictly rational. On this way of seeing the two disciplines, if at least one of the premises of an argument is derived from revelation, the argument falls in the domain of theology; otherwise it falls into philosophy's domain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_ChristianPhilosophy_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_ChristianPhilosophy-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inwagen_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inwagen-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rejections_of_metaphysics">Rejections of metaphysics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Rejections of metaphysics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>Meta-metaphysics</b> is the branch of philosophy that is concerned with the foundations of metaphysics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> A number of individuals have suggested that much or all of metaphysics should be rejected, a meta-metaphysical position known as <b>metaphysical deflationism</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> or <b>ontological deflationism</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> rejected <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholastic</a> metaphysics, and argued strongly for what is now called <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>, being seen later as the father of modern empirical science. In the 18th century, David Hume took a strong position, arguing that all genuine knowledge involves either mathematics or matters of fact and that metaphysics, which goes beyond these, is worthless. He concluded his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enquiry_Concerning_Human_Understanding" title="Enquiry Concerning Human Understanding">Enquiry Concerning Human Understanding</a></i> (1748) with the statement:
</p>
<blockquote><p>If we take in our hand any volume [book]; of divinity or school metaphysics, for instance; let us ask, <i>Does it contain any abstract reasoning concerning quantity or number?</i> No. <i>Does it contain any experimental reasoning concerning matter of fact and existence?</i> No. Commit it then to the flames: for it can contain nothing but sophistry and illusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Thirty-three years after Hume's <i>Enquiry</i> appeared, Immanuel Kant published his <i><a href="/wiki/Critique_of_Pure_Reason" title="Critique of Pure Reason">Critique of Pure Reason</a></i>. Although he followed Hume in rejecting much of previous metaphysics, he argued that there was still room for some <a href="/wiki/Analytic%E2%80%93synthetic_distinction" title="Analytic–synthetic distinction">synthetic</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_priori" title="A priori">a priori</a></i> knowledge, concerned with matters of fact yet obtainable independent of experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> These included fundamental structures of space, time, and causality. He also argued for the freedom of the will and the existence of "things in themselves", the ultimate (but unknowable) objects of experience.
</p><p>Wittgenstein introduced the concept that metaphysics could be influenced by theories of aesthetics, via <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, vis. a world composed of "atomical facts".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1930s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.J._Ayer" title="A.J. Ayer">A.J. Ayer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Carnap" title="Rudolf Carnap">Rudolf Carnap</a> endorsed Hume's position; Carnap quoted the passage above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> They argued that metaphysical statements are neither true nor false but meaningless since, according to their <a href="/wiki/Verificationism" title="Verificationism">verifiability theory of meaning</a>, a statement is meaningful only if there can be empirical evidence for or against it. Thus, while Ayer rejected the monism of Spinoza, he avoided a commitment to <a href="/wiki/Pluralism_(philosophy)" title="Pluralism (philosophy)">pluralism</a>, the contrary position, by holding both views to be without <a href="/wiki/Meaning_(philosophy)" title="Meaning (philosophy)">meaning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> Carnap took a similar line with the controversy over the reality of the external world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> While the logical positivism movement is now considered dead (with Ayer, a major proponent, admitting in a 1979 TV interview that "nearly all of it was false"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanfling_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hanfling-59">[58]</a></sup> it has continued to influence philosophy development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arguing against such rejections, the Scholastic philosopher <a href="/wiki/Edward_Feser" title="Edward Feser">Edward Feser</a> held that Hume's critique of metaphysics, and specifically <a href="/wiki/Hume%27s_fork" title="Hume's fork">Hume's fork</a>, is "notoriously self-refuting".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Feser argues that Hume's fork itself is not a conceptual truth and is not empirically testable.
</p><p>Some living philosophers, such as <a href="/wiki/Amie_Thomasson" title="Amie Thomasson">Amie Thomasson</a>, have argued that many metaphysical questions can be dissolved just by looking at the way words are used; others, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ted_Sider" title="Ted Sider">Ted Sider</a>, have argued that metaphysical questions are substantive, and that progress can be made toward answering them by comparing theories according to a range of theoretical virtues inspired by the sciences, such as simplicity and explanatory power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_schools_of_metaphysics">History and schools of metaphysics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: History and schools of metaphysics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-history">Pre-history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Pre-history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive_archeology" title="Cognitive archeology">Cognitive archeology</a> such as analysis of cave paintings and other pre-historic art and customs suggests that a form of <a href="/wiki/Perennial_philosophy" title="Perennial philosophy">perennial philosophy</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamanic" title="Shamanic">Shamanic</a> metaphysics may stretch back to the birth of <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">behavioral modernity</a>, all around the world. Similar beliefs are found in present-day "stone age" cultures such as Australian <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_Australians" title="Aboriginal Australians">aboriginals</a>. Perennial philosophy postulates the existence of a spirit or concept world alongside the day-to-day world, and interactions between these worlds during dreaming and ritual, or on special days or at special places. It has been argued that perennial philosophy formed the basis for <a href="/wiki/Platonism" title="Platonism">Platonism</a>, with Plato articulating, rather than creating, much older widespread beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age">Bronze Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Bronze Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bronze Age cultures such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Mesopotamia" title="Ancient Mesopotamia">ancient Mesopotamia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a> (along with similarly structured but chronologically later cultures such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mayans" title="Mayans">Mayans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aztecs" title="Aztecs">Aztecs</a>) developed belief systems based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a>, anthropomorphic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gods" title="Gods">gods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_dualism" title="Mind–body dualism">mind–body dualism</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Spirit_world_(Spiritualism)" title="Spirit world (Spiritualism)">spirit world</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> to explain causes and cosmology. These cultures appear to have been interested in <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and may have associated or identified the stars with some of these entities. In ancient Egypt, the ontological distinction between order (<a href="/wiki/Maat" title="Maat">maat</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaos_(mythology)" title="Chaos (mythology)">chaos</a> (<a href="/wiki/Isfet_(Egyptian_mythology)" title="Isfet (Egyptian mythology)">Isfet</a>) seems to have been important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Socratic_Greece">Pre-Socratic Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Pre-Socratic Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monad.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Monad.svg/220px-Monad.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Monad.svg/330px-Monad.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Monad.svg/440px-Monad.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monad.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The circled dot was used by the Pythagoreans and later Greeks to represent the first metaphysical being, the <a href="/wiki/Monad_(philosophy)" title="Monad (philosophy)">Monad</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Absolute" title="The Absolute">The Absolute</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The first named Greek philosopher, according to <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales</a> of <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">Miletus</a>, early 6th century BCE. He made use of purely physical explanations to explain the phenomena of the world rather than the mythological and divine explanations of tradition. He is thought to have posited water as the single underlying principle (or <i><a href="/wiki/Arche" title="Arche">arche</a></i> in later <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian</a> terminology) of the material world. His fellow, but younger Miletians, <a href="/wiki/Anaximander" title="Anaximander">Anaximander</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anaximenes_of_Miletus" title="Anaximenes of Miletus">Anaximenes</a>, also posited monistic underlying principles, namely <i><a href="/wiki/Apeiron" title="Apeiron">apeiron</a></i> (the indefinite or boundless) and air respectively.
</p><p>Another school was the <a href="/wiki/Eleatics" title="Eleatics">Eleatics</a>, in southern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. The group was founded in the early fifth century BCE by <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a>, and included <a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Elea" title="Zeno of Elea">Zeno of Elea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melissus_of_Samos" title="Melissus of Samos">Melissus of Samos</a>. Methodologically, the Eleatics were broadly rationalist, and took logical standards of clarity and necessity to be the criteria of <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a>. Parmenides' chief doctrine was that reality is a single unchanging and universal Being. Zeno used <i><a href="/wiki/Reductio_ad_absurdum" title="Reductio ad absurdum">reductio ad absurdum</a></i>, to demonstrate the illusory nature of change and time in his <a href="/wiki/Zeno%27s_paradoxes" title="Zeno's paradoxes">paradoxes</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, in contrast, made change central, teaching that "all things flow". His philosophy, expressed in brief aphorisms, is quite cryptic. For instance, he also taught the <a href="/wiki/Unity_of_opposites" title="Unity of opposites">unity of opposites</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> and his teacher <a href="/wiki/Leucippus" title="Leucippus">Leucippus</a>, are known for formulating an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_theory" title="Atomic theory">atomic theory</a> for the cosmos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes87_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes87-66">[65]</a></sup> They are considered forerunners of the scientific method.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_China">Classical China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Classical China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yin_yang.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Yin_yang.svg/230px-Yin_yang.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Yin_yang.svg/345px-Yin_yang.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Yin_yang.svg/460px-Yin_yang.svg.png 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yin_yang.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The modern "yin and yang symbol" (<i><a href="/wiki/Taijitu" title="Taijitu">taijitu</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>Metaphysics in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophy</a> can be traced back to the earliest Chinese philosophical concepts from the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a> (Heaven) and <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">yin and yang</a>. The fourth century BCE saw a turn towards <a href="/wiki/Cosmogony" title="Cosmogony">cosmogony</a> with the rise of <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> (in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daodejing" title="Daodejing">Daodejing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi</a>) and sees the natural world as dynamic and constantly changing processes which spontaneously arise from a single immanent metaphysical source or principle (<a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">Tao</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-67">[66]</a></sup> Another philosophical school which arose around this time was the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Naturalists" title="School of Naturalists">School of Naturalists</a> which saw the ultimate metaphysical principle as the <a href="/wiki/Taiji_(philosophy)" title="Taiji (philosophy)">Taiji</a>, the "supreme polarity" composed of the forces of yin and yang which were always in a state of change seeking balance. Another concern of Chinese metaphysics, especially Taoism, is the relationship and nature of being and non-being (<i>you</i> 有 and <i>wu</i> 無). The Taoists held that the ultimate, the Tao, was also non-being or no-presence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-67">[66]</a></sup> Other important concepts were those of spontaneous generation or natural vitality (<a href="/wiki/Ziran" title="Ziran">Ziran</a>) and "correlative resonance" (<a href="/wiki/Ganying" title="Ganying">Ganying</a>).
</p><p>After the fall of the Han dynasty (220 CE), China saw the rise of the Neo-Taoist <a href="/wiki/Xuanxue" title="Xuanxue">Xuanxue</a> school. This school was very influential in developing the concepts of later Chinese metaphysics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-67">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy" title="Buddhist philosophy">Buddhist philosophy</a> entered China (c. 1st century) and was influenced by the native Chinese metaphysical concepts to develop new theories. The native <a href="/wiki/Tiantai" title="Tiantai">Tiantai</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huayen" title="Huayen">Huayen</a> schools of philosophy maintained and reinterpreted the Indian theories of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shunyata" title="Shunyata">shunyata</a></i> (emptiness, <i>kong</i> 空) and <a href="/wiki/Buddha-nature" title="Buddha-nature">Buddha-nature</a> (<i>Fo xing</i> 佛性) into the theory of <a href="/wiki/Huayan#Interpenetration" title="Huayan">interpenetration</a> of phenomena. <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucians</a> like <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Zai" title="Zhang Zai">Zhang Zai</a> under the influence of other schools developed the concepts of "principle" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_(Neo-Confucianism)" title="Li (Neo-Confucianism)">li</a>) and vital energy (<i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Greece">Classical Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Classical Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Socrates_and_Plato">Socrates and Plato</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Socrates and Plato">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> is known for his <a href="/wiki/Dialectic" title="Dialectic">dialectic</a> or questioning approach to philosophy rather than a positive metaphysical doctrine.
</p><p>His pupil, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> is famous for his theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonic_forms" title="Platonic forms">forms</a> (which he places in the mouth of Socrates in his dialogues). <a href="/wiki/Platonic_realism" title="Platonic realism">Platonic realism</a> (also considered a form of idealism)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> is considered to be a solution to the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>; i.e., what particular objects have in common is that they share a specific Form which is universal to all others of their respective kind.
</p><p>The theory has a number of other aspects:
</p>
<ul><li>Epistemological: knowledge of the Forms is more certain than mere sensory data.</li>
<li>Ethical: The Form of the Good sets an objective standard for morality.</li>
<li>Time and Change: The world of the Forms is eternal and unchanging. Time and change belong only to the lower sensory world. "Time is a moving image of Eternity".</li>
<li>Abstract objects and mathematics: Numbers, geometrical figures, etc., exist mind-independently in the World of Forms.</li></ul>
<p>Platonism developed into <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>, a philosophy with a monotheistic and mystical flavour that survived well into the early Christian era.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aristotle">Aristotle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metaphysics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Aristotle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Plato's pupil <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> wrote widely on almost every subject, including <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">metaphysics</a>. His solution to the problem of universals contrasts with Plato's. Whereas Platonic Forms are existentially apparent in the visible world, Aristotelian <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essences</a> dwell in particulars.
